	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Sex Pistols</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the yaoi manga titled <i>Sex Pistols</i> in Japan, see <a href="/wiki/Love_Pistols" title="Love Pistols">Love Pistols</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">British punk rock band</div> 
<p class="mw-empty-elt">


</p>
<table class="infobox vcard plainlist" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;background-color: #b0c4de"><div style="display:inline;" class="fn org">Sex Pistols</div></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Sex_Pistols_in_Paradiso.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Sex Pistols in Paradiso.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Sex_Pistols_in_Paradiso.jpg/300px-Sex_Pistols_in_Paradiso.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Sex_Pistols_in_Paradiso.jpg/450px-Sex_Pistols_in_Paradiso.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Sex_Pistols_in_Paradiso.jpg/600px-Sex_Pistols_in_Paradiso.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3676" data-file-height="2448" /></a><div>The Sex Pistols in Amsterdam in 1977 (L–R: Paul Cook, Glen Matlock, John Lydon and Steve Jones)</div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: #b0c4de">Background information</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Origin</th><td><a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, England</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Genres</th><td><a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">Punk rock</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><span class="nowrap">Years active</span></th><td><div class="hlist">

<p>The <b>Sex Pistols</b> were an English <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">punk rock</a> band that formed in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> in 1975. They were responsible for initiating the <a href="/wiki/Punk_subculture" title="Punk subculture">punk movement</a> in the United Kingdom and inspiring many later punk and <a href="/wiki/Alternative_rock" title="Alternative rock">alternative rock</a> musicians. Although their initial career lasted just two and a half years and produced only four singles and one studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/Never_Mind_the_Bollocks,_Here%27s_the_Sex_Pistols" title="Never Mind the Bollocks, Here&#39;s the Sex Pistols">Never Mind the Bollocks, Here's the Sex Pistols</a></i>, they are regarded as one of the most influential acts in the history of <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">popular music</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RRHF_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RRHF-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-S&amp;S_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S&amp;S-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sex Pistols originally comprised vocalist <a href="/wiki/John_Lydon" title="John Lydon">Johnny Rotten</a> (John Lydon), guitarist <a href="/wiki/Steve_Jones_(musician)" title="Steve Jones (musician)">Steve Jones</a>, drummer <a href="/wiki/Paul_Cook" title="Paul Cook">Paul Cook</a> and bassist <a href="/wiki/Glen_Matlock" title="Glen Matlock">Glen Matlock</a>. Matlock was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Sid_Vicious" title="Sid Vicious">Sid Vicious</a> in early 1977. Under the management of <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_McLaren" title="Malcolm McLaren">Malcolm McLaren</a>, the band attracted controversies that both captivated and appalled Britain. Through an obscenity-laced television interview in December 1976 and their May 1977 single "<a href="/wiki/God_Save_the_Queen_(Sex_Pistols_song)" title="God Save the Queen (Sex Pistols song)">God Save the Queen</a>", attacking Britons' social conformity and deference to the Crown, they precipitated the punk rock movement.
</p><p>In January 1978, at the end of an over-hyped and turbulent tour of the United States, Rotten announced the band's break-up. Over the next few months, the three remaining band members recorded songs for McLaren's film version of the Sex Pistols' story, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Rock_%27n%27_Roll_Swindle" title="The Great Rock &#39;n&#39; Roll Swindle">The Great Rock 'n' Roll Swindle</a></i>. Vicious died of a <a href="/wiki/Heroin_overdose" class="mw-redirect" title="Heroin overdose">heroin overdose</a> in February 1979, following his arrest for the alleged <a href="/wiki/Sid_Vicious#Nancy_Spungen.27s_death_and_Sid_Vicious.27s_arrest" title="Sid Vicious">murder</a> of his <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Spungen" title="Nancy Spungen">girlfriend</a>.
</p><p>Rotten, Jones, Cook and Matlock briefly reunited for a concert tour in 1996. On 24 February 2006, the Sex Pistols—the four original members plus Vicious—were inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame" title="Rock and Roll Hall of Fame">Rock and Roll Hall of Fame</a>, but they refused to attend the ceremony, calling the museum "a piss stain".<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex_Pistols&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_early_days">Origins and early days</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex_Pistols&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins and early days">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Sex Pistols evolved from the Strand, a London band formed in 1972 with working-class teenagers <a href="/wiki/Steve_Jones_(musician)" title="Steve Jones (musician)">Steve Jones</a> on vocals, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Cook" title="Paul Cook">Paul Cook</a> on drums and <a href="/wiki/Wally_Nightingale" title="Wally Nightingale">Wally Nightingale</a> on guitar. According to a later account by Jones, both he and Cook played on instruments they had stolen.<sup id="cite_ref-SJint_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SJint-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Early line-ups of the Strand—sometimes known as the Swankers—also included Jim Mackin on organ and Stephen Hayes (and later, briefly, Del Noones) on bass.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The band members regularly hung out at two clothing shops on the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Road" title="King&#39;s Road">King's Road</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chelsea,_London" title="Chelsea, London">Chelsea</a>, London: John Krivine and Steph Raynor's <a href="/wiki/Acme_Attractions" title="Acme Attractions">Acme Attractions</a> (where <a href="/wiki/Don_Letts" title="Don Letts">Don Letts</a> worked as manager)<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_McLaren" title="Malcolm McLaren">Malcolm McLaren</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vivienne_Westwood" title="Vivienne Westwood">Vivienne Westwood</a>'s Too Fast to Live, Too Young to Die. McLaren's and Westwood's shop had opened in 1971 as Let It Rock, with a 1950s revival <a href="/wiki/Teddy_Boy" title="Teddy Boy">Teddy Boy</a> theme. It had been renamed in 1972 to focus on another revival trend, the <a href="/wiki/Rocker_(subculture)" title="Rocker (subculture)">rocker</a> look associated with <a href="/wiki/Marlon_Brando" title="Marlon Brando">Marlon Brando</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/John_Lydon" title="John Lydon">John Lydon</a> later observed, "Malcolm and Vivienne were really a pair of shysters: they would sell anything to any trend that they could grab onto."<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The shop became a focal point of the punk rock scene, bringing together participants such as the future <a href="/wiki/Sid_Vicious" title="Sid Vicious">Sid Vicious</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marco_Pirroni" title="Marco Pirroni">Marco Pirroni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gene_October" title="Gene October">Gene October</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Stewart_(musician)" title="Mark Stewart (musician)">Mark Stewart</a>, among many others.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pamela_Rooke" title="Pamela Rooke">Jordan</a>, the wildly styled shop assistant, is credited with "pretty well single-handedly paving the punk look".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In early 1974, Jones asked McLaren to manage the Strand. Effectively agreeing, McLaren paid for their first formal rehearsal space. <a href="/wiki/Glen_Matlock" title="Glen Matlock">Glen Matlock</a>, an art student who occasionally worked at Too Fast to Live, Too Young to Die, was recruited as the band's regular bassist.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In November, McLaren temporarily relocated to New York City. Before his departure, McLaren and Westwood had conceived a new identity for their shop: renamed <a href="/wiki/SEX_(boutique)" class="mw-redirect" title="SEX (boutique)">Sex</a>, it changed its focus from retro couture to <a href="/wiki/Sadomasochism" title="Sadomasochism">S&amp;M</a>-inspired "anti-fashion", with a billing as "Specialists in rubberwear, glamourwear &amp; stagewear".<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> After informally managing and promoting <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Dolls" class="mw-redirect" title="The New York Dolls">the New York Dolls</a> for a few months, McLaren returned to London in May 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock#New_York_City" title="Punk rock">punk scene</a> that was emerging in <a href="/wiki/Lower_Manhattan" title="Lower Manhattan">Lower Manhattan</a>—in particular by the radical visual style and attitude of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hell" title="Richard Hell">Richard Hell</a>, then with <a href="/wiki/Television_(band)" title="Television (band)">Television</a>—McLaren began taking a greater interest in the Strand.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The group had been rehearsing regularly, overseen by McLaren's friend <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Rhodes" title="Bernard Rhodes">Bernard Rhodes</a>, and had performed publicly for the first time. Soon after McLaren's return, Nightingale was kicked out of the band and Jones, uncomfortable as frontman, took over guitar duties.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> According to journalist and former McLaren employee Phil Strongman, around this time the band adopted the name QT Jones and the Sex Pistols (or QT Jones &amp; His Sex Pistols, as one Rhodes-designed T-shirt put it).<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> McLaren had been talking with the New York Dolls' <a href="/wiki/Sylvain_Sylvain" title="Sylvain Sylvain">Sylvain Sylvain</a> about coming over to England to front the group. When those plans fell through, McLaren, Rhodes and the band began looking locally for a new member to assume the lead vocal duties.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> As described by Matlock, "Everyone had long hair then, even the milkman, so what we used to do was if someone had short hair we would stop them in the street and ask them if they fancied themselves as a singer.".<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> For instance, former singer with boy band <a href="/wiki/Slik" title="Slik">Slik</a> and future <a href="/wiki/Ultravox" title="Ultravox">Ultravox</a> front man <a href="/wiki/Midge_Ure" title="Midge Ure">Midge Ure</a> claims to have been approached by McLaren, but to have refused the offer.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> With the search going nowhere, McLaren made several calls to Richard Hell, who turned down the invitation.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="John_Lydon_joins_the_band">John Lydon joins the band</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex_Pistols&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: John Lydon joins the band">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In August 1975, Rhodes spotted nineteen-year-old King's Road habitué John Lydon wearing a <a href="/wiki/Pink_Floyd" title="Pink Floyd">Pink Floyd</a> T-shirt with the words <i>I Hate</i> handwritten above the band's name and holes scratched through the eyes.<sup id="cite_ref-JL74_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JL74-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rstn_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rstn-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Reports vary at this point: the same day, or soon after, either Rhodes or McLaren asked Lydon to come to a nearby pub in the evening to meet Jones and Cook.<sup id="cite_ref-JL74_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JL74-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> According to Jones, "He came in with green hair. I thought he had a really interesting face. I liked his look. He had his 'I Hate Pink Floyd' T-shirt on, and it was held together with safety pins. John had something special, but when he started talking he was a real arsehole—but smart."<sup id="cite_ref-JL74_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JL74-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> When the pub closed, the group moved on to Sex, where Lydon, who had given little thought to singing, was convinced to improvise along to <a href="/wiki/Alice_Cooper" title="Alice Cooper">Alice Cooper</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/I%27m_Eighteen" title="I&#39;m Eighteen">I'm Eighteen</a>" on the shop jukebox. Though the performance drove the band members to laughter, McLaren convinced them to start rehearsing with Lydon.<sup id="cite_ref-JL74_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JL74-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lydon later described the social context in which the band came together:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Early Seventies Britain was a very depressing place. It was completely run-down, there was trash on the streets, total unemployment—just about everybody was on strike. Everybody was brought up with an education system that told you point blank that if you came from the wrong side of the tracks...then you had no hope in hell and no career prospects at all. Out of that came pretentious <i>moi</i> and the Sex Pistols and then a whole bunch of copycat wankers after us.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Journalist <a href="/wiki/Nick_Kent" title="Nick Kent">Nick Kent</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Musical_Express" class="mw-redirect" title="New Musical Express">New Musical Express</a></i> (<i>NME</i>) jammed occasionally with the band, but left upon Lydon's recruitment. "When I came along, I took one look at him and said, 'No.&#160;That has to go,'" Lydon later explained. "He's never written a good word about me ever since."<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In September, McLaren again helped hire private rehearsal space for the group, who had been practising in pubs. Cook, who had a full-time job he was loath to give up, was making noises about quitting. According to Matlock's later description, Cook "created a smokescreen" by claiming Jones was not skilled enough to be the band's sole guitarist. An advertisement was placed in <i><a href="/wiki/Melody_Maker" title="Melody Maker">Melody Maker</a></i> for a "Whizz Kid Guitarist. Not older than 20. Not worse looking than <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Thunders" title="Johnny Thunders">Johnny Thunders</a>" (referring to a leading member of the New York punk scene).<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Most of those who auditioned were incompetent, but in McLaren's view, the process created a new sense of solidarity among the four band members.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Steve_New" title="Steve New">Steve New</a> was considered the one talented guitarist to have tried out and the band invited him to join. Jones was improving rapidly, however, and the band's developing sound had no room for the technical lead work at which New was adept. He departed after a month.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lydon had been rechristened "Johnny Rotten" by Jones, apparently because of his bad dental hygiene.<sup id="cite_ref-rstn_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rstn-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The band also settled on a name. After considering options such as Le Bomb, Subterraneans, the Damned, Beyond, Teenage Novel, Kid Gladlove, and Crème de la Crème, they decided on Sex Pistols—a shortened form of the name they had apparently been working under informally.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> McLaren later said the name derived "from the idea of a pistol, a pin-up, a young thing, a better-looking assassin". Not given to modesty, false or otherwise, he added: "[I] launched the idea in the form of a band of kids who could be perceived as being bad."<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The group began writing original material: Rotten was the lyricist and Matlock the primary melody writer (though their first collaboration, "<a href="/wiki/Pretty_Vacant" title="Pretty Vacant">Pretty Vacant</a>", had a complete lyric by Matlock, which Rotten tweaked a bit); official credit was shared equally among the four.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SR89_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SR89-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sex_Pistols,_August_1975.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a0/Sex_Pistols%2C_August_1975.jpg/220px-Sex_Pistols%2C_August_1975.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a0/Sex_Pistols%2C_August_1975.jpg/330px-Sex_Pistols%2C_August_1975.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/a/a0/Sex_Pistols%2C_August_1975.jpg 2x" data-file-width="388" data-file-height="256" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sex_Pistols,_August_1975.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Sex Pistols in late 1975; L-R: Rotten, Matlock, Jones, and Cook</div></div></div>
<p>Their first gig was arranged by Matlock, who was studying at <a href="/wiki/Saint_Martin%27s_School_of_Art" title="Saint Martin&#39;s School of Art">Saint Martins College</a>. The band played at the school on 6 November 1975,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> in support of a <a href="/wiki/Pub_rock_(United_Kingdom)" title="Pub rock (United Kingdom)">pub rock</a> group called <a href="/wiki/Bazooka_Joe_(band)" title="Bazooka Joe (band)">Bazooka Joe</a>, arranging to use their amps and drums. The Sex Pistols performed several cover songs, including <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">the Who</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Substitute_(The_Who_song)" title="Substitute (The Who song)">Substitute</a>", the <a href="/wiki/Small_Faces" title="Small Faces">Small Faces</a>' "<a href="/wiki/Whatcha_Gonna_Do_About_It" title="Whatcha Gonna Do About It">Whatcha Gonna Do About It</a>", and "<a href="/wiki/(I%27m_Not_Your)_Steppin%27_Stone" title="(I&#39;m Not Your) Steppin&#39; Stone">(I'm Not Your) Steppin' Stone</a>", made famous by <a href="/wiki/The_Monkees" title="The Monkees">the Monkees</a>; according to observers, they were unexceptional musically aside from being extremely loud. Before the Pistols could play the few original songs they had written to date, Bazooka Joe pulled the plugs as they saw their gear being trashed. A brief physical altercation between members of the two bands took place on stage.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Building_a_following">Building a following</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex_Pistols&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Building a following">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Saint Martins gig was followed by other performances at colleges and art schools around London. The Sex Pistols' core group of followers—including <a href="/wiki/Siouxsie_Sioux" title="Siouxsie Sioux">Siouxsie Sioux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steven_Severin" title="Steven Severin">Steven Severin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Billy_Idol" title="Billy Idol">Billy Idol</a>, who eventually formed bands of their own, as well as Jordan and <a href="/wiki/Soo_Catwoman" title="Soo Catwoman">Soo Catwoman</a>—came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Bromley_Contingent" title="Bromley Contingent">Bromley Contingent</a>, after the suburban borough several were from.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Their cutting-edge fashion, much of it supplied by Sex, ignited a trend that was adopted by the new fans the band attracted.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> McLaren and Westwood saw the incipient London punk movement as a vehicle for more than just couture. They were both captivated by the <a href="/wiki/May_1968_in_France" class="mw-redirect" title="May 1968 in France">May 1968 radical uprising in Paris</a>, particularly by the ideology and agitations of the <a href="/wiki/Situationists" class="mw-redirect" title="Situationists">Situationists</a>, as well as the anarchist thought of <a href="/wiki/Buenaventura_Durruti" title="Buenaventura Durruti">Buenaventura Durruti</a> and others.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RotJonesMatCook.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1a/RotJonesMatCook.jpg/290px-RotJonesMatCook.jpg" decoding="async" width="290" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/1a/RotJonesMatCook.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="398" data-file-height="250" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RotJonesMatCook.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The original line-up of the Sex Pistols, early 1976. Left to right: Rotten, Jones, Matlock and Cook.</div></div></div>
<p>These interests were shared with <a href="/wiki/Jamie_Reid" title="Jamie Reid">Jamie Reid</a>, an old friend of McLaren who began producing publicity material for the Sex Pistols in the spring of 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The cut-up lettering employed to create the classic Sex Pistols logo and many subsequent designs for the band was actually introduced by McLaren's friend Helen Wallington-Lloyd.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> "We used to talk to John [Lydon] a lot about the Situationists," Reid later said. "The Sex Pistols seemed the perfect vehicle to communicate ideas directly to people who weren't getting the message from left-wing politics."<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> McLaren was also arranging for the band's first photo sessions.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> As described by music historian <a href="/wiki/Jon_Savage" title="Jon Savage">Jon Savage</a>, "With his green hair, hunched stance and ragged look, [Lydon] looked like a cross between <a href="/wiki/Uriah_Heep" title="Uriah Heep">Uriah Heep</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hell" title="Richard Hell">Richard Hell</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first Sex Pistols gig to attract broader attention was as a supporting act for <a href="/wiki/Eddie_and_the_Hot_Rods" title="Eddie and the Hot Rods">Eddie and the Hot Rods</a>, a leading pub rock group, at the <a href="/wiki/Marquee_Club" title="Marquee Club">Marquee</a> on 12 February 1976. Rotten "was now really pushing the barriers of performance, walking off stage, sitting with the audience, throwing Jordan across the dance floor and chucking chairs around, before smashing some of Eddie and the Hot Rods' gear."<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The band's first review appeared in the <i>NME</i>, accompanied by a brief interview in which Steve Jones declared, "Actually we're not into music. We're into chaos."<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Among those who read the article were two students at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bolton" title="University of Bolton">Bolton Institute of Technology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Howard_Devoto" title="Howard Devoto">Howard Devoto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pete_Shelley" title="Pete Shelley">Pete Shelley</a>, who headed down to London in search of the Sex Pistols. After chatting with McLaren at Sex, they saw the band at a couple of late February gigs.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The two friends immediately began organising their own Pistols-style group, the <a href="/wiki/Buzzcocks" title="Buzzcocks">Buzzcocks</a>. As Devoto later put it, "My life changed the moment that I saw the Sex Pistols."<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Pistols were soon playing other important venues, debuting at <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Street" title="Oxford Street">Oxford Street</a>'s <a href="/wiki/100_Club" title="100 Club">100 Club</a> on 30 March.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> On 3 April, they played for the first time at the Nashville, supporting <a href="/wiki/The_101ers" title="The 101ers">the 101ers</a>. The pub rock group's lead singer, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Strummer" title="Joe Strummer">Joe Strummer</a>, saw the Pistols for the first time that night—and recognised punk rock as the future.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> A return gig at the Nashville on 23 April demonstrated the band's growing musical competence, but by all accounts lacked a spark. Westwood provided that by instigating a fight with another audience member; McLaren and Rotten were soon involved in the melee.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Cook later said, "That fight at the Nashville: that's when all the publicity got hold of it and the violence started creeping in.... I think everybody was ready to go and we were the catalyst."<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The Pistols were soon banned from both the Nashville and the Marquee.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 23 April, as well, the <a href="/wiki/Ramones_(album)" title="Ramones (album)">debut album</a> by the leading punk rock band in the New York scene, the <a href="/wiki/Ramones" title="Ramones">Ramones</a>, was released. Though it is regarded as seminal to the growth of punk rock in England and elsewhere, Lydon has repeatedly rejected any suggestion that it influenced the Sex Pistols: "[The Ramones] were all long-haired and of no interest to me. I didn't like their image, what they stood for, or anything about them";<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> "They were hilarious but you can only go so far with 'duh-dur-dur-duh'. I've heard it. Next. Move on."<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>  On 11 May, the Pistols began a four-week-long Tuesday night residency at the 100 Club.<sup id="cite_ref-GG30_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GG30-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>  They devoted the rest of the month to touring small cities and towns in the north of England and recording demos in London with producer and recording artist <a href="/wiki/Chris_Spedding" title="Chris Spedding">Chris Spedding</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GG30_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GG30-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The following month they played their first gig in <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>, arranged by Devoto and Shelley. The Sex Pistols' 4 June performance at the <a href="/wiki/Free_Trade_Hall" title="Free Trade Hall">Lesser Free Trade Hall</a> set off a punk rock boom in the city.<sup id="cite_ref-SPG_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPG-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 4 and 6 July, respectively, two newly formed London punk rock acts, <a href="/wiki/The_Clash" title="The Clash">the Clash</a>—with Strummer as lead vocalist—and <a href="/wiki/The_Damned_(band)" title="The Damned (band)">the Damned</a>, made their live debuts opening for the Sex Pistols. On their off night in between, the Pistols (despite Lydon's later professed disdain) showed up for a Ramones gig at <a href="/wiki/Dingwalls" title="Dingwalls">Dingwalls</a>, like virtually everyone else at the heart of the London punk scene.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> During a return Manchester engagement, 20 July, the Pistols premiered a new song, "<a href="/wiki/Anarchy_in_the_U.K." title="Anarchy in the U.K.">Anarchy in the U.K.</a>", reflecting elements of the radical ideologies to which Rotten was being exposed. According to Jon Savage, "there seems little doubt that Lydon was fed material by Vivienne Westwood and Jamie Reid, which he then converted into his own lyric."<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> "Anarchy in the U.K." was among the seven originals recorded in another demo session that month, this one overseen by the band's sound engineer, <a href="/wiki/Dave_Goodman_(record_producer)" title="Dave Goodman (record producer)">Dave Goodman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> McLaren organized a major event for 29 August at the <a href="/wiki/The_Screen_On_The_Green" title="The Screen On The Green">Screen on the Green</a> in London's <a href="/wiki/Islington" title="Islington">Islington</a> district: the Buzzcocks and The Clash opened for the Sex Pistols in punk's "first metropolitan test of strength".<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Three days later, the band were in Manchester to tape what was their first television appearance, for <a href="/wiki/Tony_Wilson" title="Tony Wilson">Tony Wilson</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/So_It_Goes_(TV_series)" title="So It Goes (TV series)">So It Goes</a></i>. Scheduled to perform just one song, "Anarchy in the U.K.", the band ran straight through another two numbers as pandemonium broke out in the control room.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sex Pistols played their first concert outside Britain on 3 September, at the opening of the Chalet du Lac disco in Paris. The Bromley Contingent made the trip and Siouxsie Sioux was hassled by locals due to her outfit with bare breasts.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The following day, the <i>So It Goes</i> performance aired; the audience heard "Anarchy in the U.K." introduced with a shout of "Get off your arse!"<sup id="cite_ref-G37_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G37-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> On 13 September, the Pistols began a tour of Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> A week later, back in London, they headlined the opening night of the <a href="/wiki/100_Club_Punk_Festival" class="mw-redirect" title="100 Club Punk Festival">100 Club Punk Special</a>. Organised by McLaren (for whom the word "festival" had too much of a hippie connotation), the event was "considered the moment that was the catalyst for the years to come."<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Belying the common perception that punk bands couldn't play their instruments, contemporary music press reviews, later critical assessments of concert recordings, and testimonials by fellow musicians indicate that the Pistols had developed into a tight, ferocious live band.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> As Rotten tested out wild vocalisation styles, the instrumentalists experimented "with overload, feedback and distortion...pushing their equipment to the limit".<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="EMI_and_the_Grundy_incident">EMI and the Grundy incident</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex_Pistols&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: EMI and the Grundy incident">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Anarchy_in_the_UK.ogg" title="File:Anarchy in the UK.ogg">"Anarchy in the U.K."</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="29.120975056689" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Anarchy_in_the_UK.ogg" data-mwprovider="local"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/3a/Anarchy_in_the_UK.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (117 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="117495" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/transcoded/3/3a/Anarchy_in_the_UK.ogg/Anarchy_in_the_UK.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="192832" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">"Anarchy in the UK" reached number 38 on the UK singles chart</div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>On 8 October 1976, the major record label <a href="/wiki/EMI" title="EMI">EMI</a> signed the Sex Pistols to a two-year contract.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In short order, the band was in the studio recording a full-dress session with Dave Goodman. As later described by Matlock, "The idea was to get the spirit of the live performance. We were pressurized to make it faster and faster."<sup id="cite_ref-JS245_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JS245-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The riotous results were rejected by the band. <a href="/wiki/Chris_Thomas_(record_producer)" title="Chris Thomas (record producer)">Chris Thomas</a>, who had produced <a href="/wiki/Roxy_Music" title="Roxy Music">Roxy Music</a> and mixed Pink Floyd's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Side_of_the_Moon" title="The Dark Side of the Moon">The Dark Side of the Moon</a></i>, was brought in to produce.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The band's first single, "Anarchy in the U.K.", was released on 26 November 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-JS245_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JS245-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Robb_(musician)" title="John Robb (musician)">John Robb</a>—soon to be a cofounder of <a href="/wiki/The_Membranes" title="The Membranes">The Membranes</a> and later a music journalist—described the record's impact: "From Steve Jones' opening salvo of descending chords, to Johnny Rotten's fantastic sneering vocals, this song is the perfect statement...a stunningly powerful piece of punk politics...a lifestyle choice, a manifesto that heralds a new era".<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Colin_Newman" title="Colin Newman">Colin Newman</a>, who had just cofounded the band <a href="/wiki/Wire_(band)" title="Wire (band)">Wire</a>, heard it as "the clarion call of a generation."<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>"Anarchy in the U.K." was not the first British punk single, pipped by The Damned's "<a href="/wiki/New_Rose" title="New Rose">New Rose</a>". "We Vibrate" had also appeared from <a href="/wiki/The_Vibrators" title="The Vibrators">The Vibrators</a>, a pub rock band formed early in 1976 that had become associated with punk—though, according to Jon Savage "with their long hair and mildly risqué name, the Vibrators were passers-by as far as punk taste-makers were concerned."<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Unlike those songs, whose lyrical content was comfortably within rock 'n' roll traditions, "Anarchy in the U.K." linked punk to a newly politicised attitude—the Pistols' stance was aggrieved, euphoric and nihilistic, all at the same time. Rotten's howls of "I am an anti-Christ" and "Destroy!" repurposed rock as an ideological weapon.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The single's packaging and visual promotion also broke new ground. Reid and McLaren came up with the notion of selling the record in a completely wordless, featureless black sleeve.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The primary image associated with the single was Reid's "anarchy flag" poster: a <a href="/wiki/Union_Flag" class="mw-redirect" title="Union Flag">Union Flag</a> ripped up and partly safety-pinned back together, with the song and band names clipped along the edges of a gaping hole in the middle. This and other images created by Reid for the Sex Pistols quickly became punk icons.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="31.323560090703" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="The_Fucking_Rotter_31_Second.ogg" data-mwprovider="local"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/40/The_Fucking_Rotter_31_Second.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (118 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="118215" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/transcoded/4/40/The_Fucking_Rotter_31_Second.ogg/The_Fucking_Rotter_31_Second.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="215312" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Fucking_Rotter_31_Second.ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Audio from the 1976 interview conducted by Bill Grundy, where Grundy is called a <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sod#English" class="extiw" title="wikt:sod">sod</a> and a <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/rotter#English" class="extiw" title="wikt:rotter">rotter</a> by Jones</div></div></div>
<p><span id="today_programme"></span>The Sex Pistols' behaviour, as much as their music, brought them national attention. On 1 December 1976, the band and members of the Bromley Contingent created a storm of publicity by swearing during an early evening live broadcast of <a href="/wiki/Thames_Television" title="Thames Television">Thames Television</a>'s <i>Today</i> programme. Appearing as last-minute replacements for fellow EMI artists <a href="/wiki/Queen_(band)" title="Queen (band)">Queen</a>, who had dropped out due to <a href="/wiki/Freddie_Mercury" title="Freddie Mercury">Freddie Mercury</a> having a dental appointment,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> the band and their entourage were offered drinks as they waited to go on air. During the interview, Jones said the band had "fucking spent" its label advance and Rotten used the word "shit". Host <a href="/wiki/Bill_Grundy" title="Bill Grundy">Bill Grundy</a>, who had earlier claimed to be drunk, engaged in repartee with <a href="/wiki/Siouxsie_Sioux" title="Siouxsie Sioux">Siouxsie Sioux</a>, who declared that she had "always wanted to meet" him. Grundy responded, "Did you really? We'll meet afterwards, shall we?" This prompted the following exchange between Jones and the host:
</p>
<dl><dd><i>Jones</i>: You dirty sod. You dirty old man.</dd>
<dd><i>Grundy</i>: Well keep going, chief, keep going. Go on. You've got another five seconds. Say something outrageous!</dd>
<dd><i>Jones</i>: You dirty bastard.</dd>
<dd><i>Grundy</i>: Go on, again.</dd>
<dd><i>Jones</i>: You dirty fucker.</dd>
<dd><i>Grundy</i>: What a clever boy(!)</dd>
<dd><i>Jones</i>: What a fucking rotter.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Filthandfury.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ef/Filthandfury.png/220px-Filthandfury.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/ef/Filthandfury.png 1.5x" data-file-width="284" data-file-height="350" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Filthandfury.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mirror" title="Daily Mirror">Daily Mirror</a></i> front page, 2 December 1976</div></div></div>
<p>Although the programme was broadcast only in the London region, the ensuing furore occupied the tabloid newspapers for days. The <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mirror" title="Daily Mirror">Daily Mirror</a></i> famously ran the headline "The Filth and the Fury!";<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> other papers such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Express" title="Daily Express">Daily Express</a></i> ("Fury at Filthy TV Chat") and the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Telegraph" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Telegraph">Daily Telegraph</a></i> ("4-Letter Words Rock TV") followed suit.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Thames Television suspended Grundy and, though he was later reinstated, the interview effectively ended his career.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The episode made the band household names throughout the country and brought punk into mainstream awareness. The Pistols set out on the Anarchy Tour of the UK, supported by the Clash and Johnny Thunders' band <a href="/wiki/The_Heartbreakers" title="The Heartbreakers">the Heartbreakers</a>, over from New York. The Damned were briefly part of the tour, before McLaren kicked them off. Media coverage was intense, and many of the concerts were cancelled by organisers or local authorities; of approximately twenty scheduled gigs, only about seven actually took place.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Following a campaign waged in the south Wales press, a crowd including carol singers and a Pentecostal preacher protested against the group outside a show in <a href="/wiki/Caerphilly" title="Caerphilly">Caerphilly</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Packers at the EMI plant refused to handle the band's single.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> London councillor Bernard Brook Partridge declared, "Most of these groups would be vastly improved by sudden death. The worst of the punk rock groups I suppose currently are the Sex Pistols. They are unbelievably nauseating. They are the antithesis of humankind. I would like to see somebody dig a very, very large, exceedingly deep hole and drop the whole bloody lot down it."<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the end of the tour in late December, three concerts were arranged in the Netherlands for January 1977. The band, hungover, boarded a plane at <a href="/wiki/London_Heathrow_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="London Heathrow Airport">London Heathrow Airport</a> early on 4 January; a few hours later, the <i><a href="/wiki/Evening_News_(London)" class="mw-redirect" title="Evening News (London)">Evening News</a></i> was reporting that the band had "vomited and spat their way" to the flight.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Despite categorical denials by the EMI representative who accompanied the group, the label, which was under political pressure, released the band from their contract.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> In one journalist's later description, the Pistols had "stoked a moral panic...precipitating the cancellation of gigs, the band’s expulsion from their EMI record deal and lurid tabloid tales of punk’s 'shock cult'".<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> As McLaren fielded offers from other labels, the band went into the studio for a round of recordings with Goodman, their last with either him or Matlock.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sid_Vicious_joins_the_band">Sid Vicious joins the band</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex_Pistols&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sid Vicious joins the band">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SexPistolsNorway1977.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/SexPistolsNorway1977.jpg/220px-SexPistolsNorway1977.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/SexPistolsNorway1977.jpg/330px-SexPistolsNorway1977.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/SexPistolsNorway1977.jpg/440px-SexPistolsNorway1977.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SexPistolsNorway1977.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Sex Pistols (Sid Vicious left, Steve Jones centre, and Johnny Rotten right) performing in Trondheim, Norway, July 1977</div></div></div>
<p>In February 1977, word leaked out that Matlock was leaving the Sex Pistols. On 28 February, McLaren sent a telegram to the <i>NME</i> confirming the split. He claimed that Matlock had been "thrown out...because he went on too long about <a href="/wiki/Paul_McCartney" title="Paul McCartney">Paul McCartney</a>.... <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a> was too much."<sup id="cite_ref-GG56_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GG56-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> In an interview a few months afterwards, Steve Jones echoed the charge that Matlock had been sacked because he "liked The Beatles".<sup id="cite_ref-SJint_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SJint-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Years later, Jones expanded on the matter of the band's issues with Matlock: "He was a good writer but he didn't look like a Sex Pistol and he was always washing his feet. His mum didn't like the songs."<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Matlock told the <i>NME</i> that he had voluntarily left the band by "mutual agreement".<sup id="cite_ref-GG56_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GG56-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Later, in his autobiography, he described the primary impetus as his increasingly acrimonious relationship with Rotten, exacerbated—in Matlock's account—by the rampant inflation of Rotten's ego "once he'd had his name in the papers".<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Lydon later claimed that "<a href="/wiki/God_Save_the_Queen_(Sex_Pistols_song)" title="God Save the Queen (Sex Pistols song)">God Save the Queen</a>", the belligerently sardonic song planned as the band's second single, had been the final straw: "[Matlock] couldn't handle those kinds of lyrics. He said it declared us fascists." Though the singer could hardly see how anti-royalism equated with fascism, he claimed, "Just to get rid of him, I didn't deny it."<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Jon Savage suggests that Rotten pushed Matlock out in an effort to demonstrate his power and autonomy from McLaren.<sup id="cite_ref-Savage,_Jon_p._308_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Savage,_Jon_p._308-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>  Matlock almost immediately formed his own band, <a href="/wiki/Rich_Kids" title="Rich Kids">Rich Kids</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Midge_Ure" title="Midge Ure">Midge Ure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steve_New" title="Steve New">Steve New</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rusty_Egan" title="Rusty Egan">Rusty Egan</a>.
</p><p>Matlock was replaced by Rotten's friend and self-appointed "ultimate Sex Pistols fan" <a href="/wiki/Sid_Vicious" title="Sid Vicious">Sid Vicious</a>. Born John Simon Ritchie, later known as John Beverley, Vicious was previously drummer of two inner circle punk bands, <a href="/wiki/Siouxsie_and_the_Banshees" title="Siouxsie and the Banshees">Siouxsie and the Banshees</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Flowers_of_Romance_(British_band)" title="The Flowers of Romance (British band)">The Flowers of Romance</a>. He was also credited with introducing the <a href="/wiki/Pogo_(dance)" title="Pogo (dance)">pogo dance</a> to the scene at the 100 Club. John Robb claims it was at the first Sex Pistols residency gig, 11 May 1976; Matlock is convinced it happened during the second night of the 100 Club Punk Special in September, when the Pistols were off playing in Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> In Matlock's description, Rotten wanted Vicious in the band because "[i]nstead of him against Steve and Paul, it would become him and Sid against Steve and Paul. He always thought of it in terms of opposing camps".<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Julien_Temple" title="Julien Temple">Julien Temple</a>, then a film student whom McLaren had put on the Sex Pistols payroll to create a comprehensive audiovisual record of the band, concurs: "Sid was John's protégé in the group, really. The other two just thought he was crazy."<sup id="cite_ref-Savage,_Jon_p._308_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Savage,_Jon_p._308-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> McLaren later stated that, much earlier in the band's career, Vivienne Westwood had told him he should "get the guy called John who came to the store a couple of times" to be the singer. When Johnny Rotten was recruited for the band, Westwood said McLaren had got it wrong: "he had got the wrong John." It was John Beverley, the future Vicious, she had been recommending.<sup id="cite_ref-Blood_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blood-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> McLaren approved the belated inclusion of Vicious, who had virtually no experience on his new instrument, on account of his look and reputation in the punk scene.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tdpe_0002_xs_Thomas_Dellert_and_the_Sex_Pistols_1978.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Tdpe_0002_xs_Thomas_Dellert_and_the_Sex_Pistols_1978.jpg/330px-Tdpe_0002_xs_Thomas_Dellert_and_the_Sex_Pistols_1978.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Tdpe_0002_xs_Thomas_Dellert_and_the_Sex_Pistols_1978.jpg/495px-Tdpe_0002_xs_Thomas_Dellert_and_the_Sex_Pistols_1978.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Tdpe_0002_xs_Thomas_Dellert_and_the_Sex_Pistols_1978.jpg/660px-Tdpe_0002_xs_Thomas_Dellert_and_the_Sex_Pistols_1978.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="552" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tdpe_0002_xs_Thomas_Dellert_and_the_Sex_Pistols_1978.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Sex Pistols with Thomas Dellert, c. 1977</div></div></div>
<p>Pogoing aside, Vicious had been involved in a notorious incident during that memorable second night of the 100 Club Punk Special. Arrested for hurling a glass at The Damned that shattered and blinded a girl in one eye, he had served time in a remand centre—and contributed to the 100 Club banning all punk bands.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> At a previous 100 Club gig, he had assaulted Nick Kent with a bicycle chain.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Indeed, McLaren's <i>NME</i> telegram said that Vicious's "best credential was he gave Nick Kent what he deserved many months ago at the Hundred Club".<sup id="cite_ref-GG56_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GG56-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> According to a later description by McLaren, "When Sid joined he couldn't play guitar but his craziness fit into the structure of the band. He was the knight in shining armour with a giant fist."<sup id="cite_ref-CR_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CR-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> "Everyone agreed he had the look," Lydon later recalled, but musical skill was another matter. "The first rehearsals...in March of 1977 with Sid were hellish.... Sid really tried hard and rehearsed a lot".<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Marco Pirroni, who had performed with Vicious in Siouxsie and the Banshees, has said, "After that, it was nothing to do with music anymore. It would just be for the sensationalism and scandal of it all. Then it became the Malcolm McLaren story".<sup id="cite_ref-CR_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CR-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Membership in the Sex Pistols had a progressively destructive effect on Vicious. As Lydon later observed, "Up to that time, Sid was absolutely childlike. Everything was fun and giggly. Suddenly he was a big pop star. Pop star status meant press, a good chance to be spotted in all the right places, adoration. That's what it all meant to Sid."<sup id="cite_ref-CR_106-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CR-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Westwood had already been feeding him material, like a tome on <a href="/wiki/Charles_Manson" title="Charles Manson">Charles Manson</a>, likely to encourage his worst instincts.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Early in 1977, he met <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Spungen" title="Nancy Spungen">Nancy Spungen</a>, an emotionally disturbed drug addict and sometime prostitute from New York.<sup id="cite_ref-CR_106-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CR-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Spungen is commonly thought to be responsible for introducing Vicious to heroin, and the emotional codependency between the couple alienated Vicious from the other members of the band. Lydon later wrote, "We did everything to get rid of Nancy.... She was killing him. I was absolutely convinced this girl was on a slow suicide mission.... Only she didn't want to go alone. She wanted to take Sid with her.... She was so utterly fucked up and evil."<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id=".22God_Save_the_Queen.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;God_Save_the_Queen&quot;">"God Save the Queen"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex_Pistols&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: &quot;God Save the Queen&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 10 March 1977, at a press ceremony held outside <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a>, the Sex Pistols publicly signed to <a href="/wiki/A%26M_Records" title="A&amp;M Records">A&amp;M Records</a> (the real signing had taken place the day before). Afterwards, intoxicated, they made their way to the A&amp;M offices. Vicious smashed in a toilet bowl and cut his foot—there is some disagreement about which happened first. As Vicious trailed blood around the offices, Rotten verbally abused the staff and Jones got frisky in the ladies' room.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> A couple of days later, the Pistols got into a rumble with another band at a club; one of Rotten's pals threatened the life of a good friend of A&amp;M's English director. On 16 March, A&amp;M broke contract with the Pistols. Twenty-five thousand copies of the planned "God Save the Queen" single, produced by Chris Thomas, had already been pressed: virtually all were destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sex_Pistols_-_God_Save_the_Queen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/dd/Sex_Pistols_-_God_Save_the_Queen.jpg/220px-Sex_Pistols_-_God_Save_the_Queen.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/dd/Sex_Pistols_-_God_Save_the_Queen.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sex_Pistols_-_God_Save_the_Queen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jamie Reid's "God Save the Queen" sleeve; in 2001, it was named the greatest record cover of all time by <i><a href="/wiki/Q_(magazine)" title="Q (magazine)">Q</a></i> magazine.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Vicious debuted with the band at London's Notre Dame Hall on 28 March.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> In May, the band signed with <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Records" title="Virgin Records">Virgin Records</a>, their third new label in little more than half a year. Virgin was more than ready to release "God Save the Queen", but new obstacles arose. Workers at the pressing plant laid down their tools in protest at the song's content. Jamie Reid's now famous cover, showing <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a> with her features obscured by the song and band names in cutout letters, offended the sleeve's plate makers.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> After much talk, production resumed and the record was finally released on 27 May.<sup id="cite_ref-May27_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-May27-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The scabrous lyrics—"God save the queen/She ain't no human being/And there's no future/In England's dreaming"—prompted widespread outcry.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Several major chains refused to stock the single.<sup id="cite_ref-May27_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-May27-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> It was banned not only by the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> but also by every independent radio station, making it the "most heavily censored record in British history".<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Rotten boasted, "We're the only honest band that's hit this planet in about two thousand million years."<sup id="cite_ref-GG70_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GG70-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>   Jones shrugged off everything the song stated and implied—or took nihilism to a logical endpoint: "I don't see how anyone could describe us as a political band. I don't even know the name of the prime minister."<sup id="cite_ref-GG70_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GG70-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The song, and its public impact, are now recognised as "punk's crowning glory".<sup id="cite_ref-O50_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O50-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Virgin release had been timed to coincide with the height of Queen Elizabeth's <a href="/wiki/Silver_Jubilee_of_Elizabeth_II" title="Silver Jubilee of Elizabeth II">Silver Jubilee</a> celebrations. By Jubilee weekend, a week and a half after the record's release, it had sold more than 150,000 copies—a massive success. On 7 June, McLaren and the record label arranged to charter a private boat and have the Sex Pistols perform while sailing down the <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">River Thames</a>, passing <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Pier" class="mw-redirect" title="Westminster Pier">Westminster Pier</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Houses of Parliament</a>. The event, a mockery of the Queen's river procession planned for two days later, ended in chaos. Police launches forced the boat to dock, and constabulary surrounded the gangplanks at the pier. While the band members and their equipment were hustled down a side stairwell, McLaren, Westwood, and many of the band's entourage were arrested.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> In critic <a href="/wiki/Sean_O%27Hagan_(journalist)" title="Sean O&#39;Hagan (journalist)">Sean O'Hagan</a>'s description, the Pistols had set off the "last and greatest outbreak of pop-based moral pandemonium".<sup id="cite_ref-O50_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O50-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:God_Save_the_Queen_Sex_Pistols_23_sec.ogg" title="File:God Save the Queen Sex Pistols 23 sec.ogg">"God Save the Queen"</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_2" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="25.722154195011" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="God_Save_the_Queen_Sex_Pistols_23_sec.ogg" data-mwprovider="local"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/2a/God_Save_the_Queen_Sex_Pistols_23_sec.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (116 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="116360" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/transcoded/2/2a/God_Save_the_Queen_Sex_Pistols_23_sec.ogg/God_Save_the_Queen_Sex_Pistols_23_sec.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="197224" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">"God Save the Queen" was originally titled "No Future", but was changed to coincide with the 1977 Jubilee</div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>With the official UK record chart for Jubilee week about to be released, the <i>Daily Mirror</i> predicted that "God Save the Queen" would be number one. As it turned out, the record placed second, behind a <a href="/wiki/Rod_Stewart" title="Rod Stewart">Rod Stewart</a> single in its fourth week at the top. Many believed that the record had actually qualified for the top spot, but that the chart had been rigged to prevent a spectacle. McLaren later claimed that <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Records" title="Columbia Records">CBS Records</a>, which was distributing both singles, told him that the Sex Pistols were actually outselling Stewart two to one. There is evidence that an exceptional directive was issued by the British Phonographic Institute, which oversaw the chart-compiling bureau, to exclude sales from record-company operated shops such as Virgin's for that week only.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Attacks on punk fans rose and in mid-June Rotten was assaulted by a knife-wielding gang outside <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Islington" title="London Borough of Islington">Islington</a>'s Pegasus pub, causing tendon damage to his left arm. Jamie Reid and Paul Cook were beaten up in other incidents; three days after the Pegasus assault, Rotten was attacked again.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> According to Cook, after the God Save The Queen single and the Bill Grundy incident, the Pistols were public enemy number one, and there was a rivalry between gangs of rockabillies or Teddie Boys and the punks which resulted in many fights. A tour of Scandinavia, planned to start at the end of the month, was delayed until mid-July. In Oslo, Lydon posed for photographs by making the Nazi salute while wearing a sweater with a Swastika.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> During the tour, a Swedish interviewer told Jones that "a lot of people" regarded the band as McLaren's "creation". Jones replied, "He's our manager, that's all. He's got nothing to do with the music or the image...he's just a good manager."<sup id="cite_ref-SJint_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SJint-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In another interview, Rotten professed bafflement at the furore surrounding the group: "I don't understand it. All we're trying to do is destroy everything."<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> At the end of August came SPOTS—Sex Pistols On Tour Secretly, a surreptitious UK tour with the band playing under pseudonyms to avoid cancellation.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>McLaren had wanted for some time to make a movie featuring the Sex Pistols. Julien Temple's first major task had been to assemble <i>Sex Pistols Number 1</i>, a 25-minute mosaic of footage from various sources, much of it refilmed by Temple from television screens.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> <i>Number 1</i> was often screened at concert venues before the band took the stage. Using media footage from the Thames incident, Temple created another propaganda-like short, <i>Jubilee Riverboat</i> (aka <i>Sex Pistols Number 2</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> During summer 1977, McLaren had been making arrangements for the feature film of his dreams, <i><a href="/wiki/Who_Killed_Bambi%3F_(unfinished_film)" title="Who Killed Bambi? (unfinished film)">Who Killed Bambi?</a></i>, to be directed by <a href="/wiki/Russ_Meyer" title="Russ Meyer">Russ Meyer</a> from a script by <a href="/wiki/Roger_Ebert" title="Roger Ebert">Roger Ebert</a>. After a single day of shooting, 11 September, production ceased when it became clear that McLaren had failed to arrange financing.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Never_Mind_the_Bollocks"><i>Never Mind the Bollocks</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex_Pistols&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Never Mind the Bollocks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Never_Mind_the_Bollocks,_Here%27s_the_Sex_Pistols" title="Never Mind the Bollocks, Here&#39;s the Sex Pistols">Never Mind the Bollocks, Here's the Sex Pistols</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WB_77-NMTB_promo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/WB_77-NMTB_promo.jpg/220px-WB_77-NMTB_promo.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="322" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/WB_77-NMTB_promo.jpg/330px-WB_77-NMTB_promo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ea/WB_77-NMTB_promo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="437" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WB_77-NMTB_promo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Promotional poster, 1977</div></div></div>
<p>Since the spring of 1977, the three senior Sex Pistols had been returning to the studio periodically with Chris Thomas to lay down the tracks for the band's debut album. Initially to be called <i>God Save Sex Pistols</i>, it became known during the summer as <i>Never Mind the Bollocks</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> According to Jones, "Sid wanted to come down and play on the album, and we tried as hard as possible not to let him anywhere near the studio. Luckily he had hepatitis at the time."<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Cook later described how many of the instrumental tracks were built up from drum and guitar parts, rather than the usual drum and bass.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Given Vicious's incompetence, Matlock had been invited to record as a session musician. In his autobiography, Matlock says he agreed to "help out", but then suggests that he cut all ties after McLaren issued the 28 February <i>NME</i> telegram announcing Matlock had been fired for liking the Beatles.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> According to Jon Savage, Matlock did play as a hired hand on 3 March, for what Savage describes as an "audition session".<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> In his autobiography, Lydon claims that Matlock's work-for-hire for his ex-band was extensive—much more so than any other source reports—seemingly to amplify a putdown: "I think I'd rather die than do something like that."<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Music historian David Howard states unambiguously that Matlock did not perform on any of the <i>Never Mind the Bollocks</i> recording sessions.<sup id="cite_ref-DH_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DH-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was Jones who ultimately played most of the bass parts on <i>Bollocks</i>;<sup id="cite_ref-Loudwire_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Loudwire-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Howard calls his rudimentary, rumbling approach the "explosive missing ingredient" of the Sex Pistols' sound.<sup id="cite_ref-DH_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DH-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Vicious's bass is reportedly present on one track that appeared on the original album release, "<a href="/wiki/Bodies_(Sex_Pistols_song)" title="Bodies (Sex Pistols song)">Bodies</a>". Jones recalls, "He played his farty old bass part and we just let him do it. When he left I dubbed another part on, leaving Sid's down low. I think it might be barely audible on the track."<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Following "God Save the Queen", two more singles were released from these sessions, "<a href="/wiki/Pretty_Vacant" title="Pretty Vacant">Pretty Vacant</a>" (largely written by Matlock) on 1 July<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> and "<a href="/wiki/Holidays_in_the_Sun_(song)" title="Holidays in the Sun (song)">Holidays in the Sun</a>" on 14 October.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Each was a Top Ten hit.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Never_Mind_the_Bollocks,_Here%27s_the_Sex_Pistols" title="Never Mind the Bollocks, Here&#39;s the Sex Pistols">Never Mind the Bollocks, Here's the Sex Pistols</a></i> was released on 28 October 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-ST69_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ST69-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> <i>Rolling Stone</i> praised the album as "just about the most exciting rock &amp; roll record of the Seventies", applauding the band for playing "with an energy and conviction that is positively transcendent in its madness and fever".<sup id="cite_ref-PN_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PN-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Some critics, disappointed that the album contained all four previously released singles, dismissed it as little more than a "greatest hits" record.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Containing both "Bodies"—in which Rotten utters "fuck" six times—and the previously censored "God Save the Queen" and featuring the word <i><a href="/wiki/Bollocks" title="Bollocks">bollocks</a></i> (popular slang for testicles) in its title, the album was banned by <a href="/wiki/Boots_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Boots Group">Boots</a>, <a href="/wiki/W._H._Smith" class="mw-redirect" title="W. H. Smith">W. H. Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Woolworths_Group" title="Woolworths Group">Woolworth's</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-JS415_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JS415-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The Conservative shadow minister for education condemned it as "a symptom of the way society is declining" and both the Independent Television Companies' Association and the Association of Independent Radio Contractors banned its advertisements.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, advance sales were sufficient to make it an undeniable number one on the  album chart.<sup id="cite_ref-JS415_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JS415-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The album title led to a legal case that attracted considerable attention: a Virgin Records store in <a href="/wiki/Nottingham" title="Nottingham">Nottingham</a> that put the album in its window was threatened with prosecution for displaying "indecent printed matter".
