The case was thrown out when defending <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Counsel" title="Queen&#39;s Counsel">QC</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Mortimer" title="John Mortimer">John Mortimer</a> produced an expert witness who established that <i>bollocks</i> was an <a href="/wiki/Old_English_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old English language">Old English</a> term for a small ball, that it appeared in place names without causing local communities erotic disturbance, and that in the nineteenth century it had been used as a nickname for clergymen: "Clergymen are known to talk a good deal of rubbish and so the word later developed the meaning of nonsense."<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> In the context of the Pistols' album title, the term does in fact primarily signify "nonsense". Steve Jones off-handedly came up with the title as the band debated what to call the album. An exasperated Jones said, "Oh, fuck it, never mind the bollocks of it all."<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After playing a few dates in the Netherlands—the beginning of a planned multinational tour—the band set out on a Never Mind the Bans tour of Britain in December 1977. Of eight scheduled dates, four were cancelled due to illness or political pressure. On Christmas Day, the Sex Pistols played two shows at Ivanhoe's in <a href="/wiki/Huddersfield" title="Huddersfield">Huddersfield</a>. Before a regular evening concert, the band performed a benefit matinee for the children of "striking firemen, laid-off workers and one-parent families."<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> These were the band's final UK performances for more than eighteen years.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="US_tour_and_the_end_of_the_band">US tour and the end of the band</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex_Pistols&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: US tour and the end of the band">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PistPost8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/17/PistPost8.jpg/290px-PistPost8.jpg" decoding="async" width="290" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/17/PistPost8.jpg/435px-PistPost8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/17/PistPost8.jpg/580px-PistPost8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="446" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PistPost8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>US poster for <i>Never Mind the Bollocks, Here's the Sex Pistols</i></div></div></div>
<p>In January 1978, the Sex Pistols embarked on a US tour, consisting mainly of dates in America's <a href="/wiki/Deep_South" title="Deep South">Deep South</a>. Originally scheduled to begin a few days before New Year's, it was delayed due to American authorities' reluctance to issue visas to band members with criminal records. Several dates in the North had to be cancelled as a result.<sup id="cite_ref-ST69_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ST69-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Though highly anticipated by fans and media, the tour was plagued by in-fighting, poor planning and physically belligerent audiences. McLaren later admitted that he purposely booked <a href="/wiki/Redneck_(stereotype)" class="mw-redirect" title="Redneck (stereotype)">redneck</a> bars to provoke hostile situations.<sup id="cite_ref-Blood_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blood-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Over the course of the two weeks, Vicious, by now heavily addicted to heroin,<sup id="cite_ref-Huey_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huey-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> began to live up to his stage name. "He finally had an audience of people who would behave with shock and horror", Lydon later wrote. "Sid was easily led by the nose."<sup id="cite_ref-JL244_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JL244-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Early in the tour, Vicious wandered off from his <a href="/wiki/Holiday_Inn" title="Holiday Inn">Holiday Inn</a> in <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis</a>, looking for drugs. When he was ultimately found, he received a beating from the security team hired by <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Records" class="mw-redirect" title="Warner Bros. Records">Warner Bros.</a>, the band's American label.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> He subsequently appeared with the words "Gimme a fix" on his chest—accounts vary as to whether the words were written or carved there.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> During a concert in <a href="/wiki/San_Antonio" title="San Antonio">San Antonio</a>, Vicious called the crowd "a bunch of faggots", before striking an audience member across the head with his bass guitar.<sup id="cite_ref-Huey_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huey-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Baton_Rouge,_Louisiana" title="Baton Rouge, Louisiana">Baton Rouge</a>, he received simulated oral sex on stage, later declaring "that's the kind of girl I like".<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Suffering from heroin withdrawal during a show in <a href="/wiki/Dallas" title="Dallas">Dallas</a>, he spat blood at a woman who had climbed onstage and punched him in the face.<sup id="cite_ref-JL244_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JL244-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> He was admitted to hospital later that night to treat various injuries. Offstage he is said to have kicked a photographer, attacked a security guard, and eventually challenged one of his own bodyguards to a fight—beaten up, he is reported to have exclaimed, "I like you. Now we can be friends."<sup id="cite_ref-CR_106-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CR-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:No_Fun_Sex_Pistols_16_sec.ogg" title="File:No Fun Sex Pistols 16 sec.ogg">"No Fun"</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_3" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="16.002448979592" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="No_Fun_Sex_Pistols_16_sec.ogg" data-mwprovider="local"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/5b/No_Fun_Sex_Pistols_16_sec.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (118 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="118266" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/transcoded/5/5b/No_Fun_Sex_Pistols_16_sec.ogg/No_Fun_Sex_Pistols_16_sec.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="198888" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">Sample of "No Fun", a cover version of <a href="/wiki/The_Stooges" title="The Stooges">the Stooges</a> song—studio recording from 1976 or 1977</div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>Rotten, meanwhile, suffering from flu<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> and coughing up blood, felt increasingly isolated from Cook and Jones, and disgusted by Vicious.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> On 14 January 1978, during the tour's final date at the <a href="/wiki/Winterland_Ballroom" title="Winterland Ballroom">Winterland Ballroom</a> in San Francisco, a disillusioned Rotten introduced the band's encore saying, "You'll get one number and one number only 'cause I'm a lazy bastard." That one number was a <a href="/wiki/The_Stooges" title="The Stooges">Stooges</a> cover, "No Fun". At the end of the song, Rotten, kneeling on the stage, chanted an unambiguous declaration, "This is no fun. No fun. This is no fun—at all. No fun." As the final cymbal crash died away, Rotten addressed the audience directly—"Ah-ha-ha. Ever get the feeling you've been cheated? Good night"—before throwing down his microphone and walking offstage.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> He later observed, "I felt cheated, and I wasn't going on with it any longer; it was a ridiculous farce. Sid was completely out of his brains—just a waste of space. The whole thing was a joke at that point.... [Malcolm] wouldn't speak to me.... He would not discuss anything with me. But then he would turn around and tell Paul and Steve that the tension was all my fault because I wouldn't agree to anything."<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 17 January, the band split, making their ways separately to Los Angeles. McLaren, Cook and Jones prepared to fly to <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a> for a working vacation. Vicious, in increasingly bad shape, was taken to Los Angeles by a friend, who then brought him to New York, where he was immediately hospitalised.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Rotten flew to New York, where he announced the band's break-up in a newspaper interview on 18 January.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Virtually broke, he telephoned the head of Virgin Records, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Branson" title="Richard Branson">Richard Branson</a>, who agreed to pay for his flight back to London, via Jamaica. In Jamaica, Branson met with members of the band <a href="/wiki/Devo" title="Devo">Devo</a>, and tried to install Rotten as their lead singer. Devo declined the offer,<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> which Rotten also found unappealing.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Cook, Jones and Vicious never performed together again live after Rotten's departure. Over the next several months, McLaren arranged for recordings in Brazil (with Jones and Cook), Paris (with Vicious) and London; each of the three and others stepped in as lead vocalists on tracks that in some cases were far from what punk was expected to sound like. These recordings were to make up the musical soundtrack for the reconceived Pistols feature film project, directed by Julien Temple, to which McLaren was now devoting himself. On 30 June, a single credited to the Sex Pistols was released: on one side, notorious criminal <a href="/wiki/Ronnie_Biggs" title="Ronnie Biggs">Ronnie Biggs</a> sang "<a href="/wiki/No_One_Is_Innocent_(song)" title="No One Is Innocent (song)">No One Is Innocent</a>" accompanied by Jones and Cook; on the other, Vicious sang the classic "<a href="/wiki/My_Way_(song)" class="mw-redirect" title="My Way (song)">My Way</a>", over both a Jones–Cook backing track and a string orchestra.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> The single reached number seven on the charts, eventually outselling all the singles with which Rotten was involved.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> McLaren was seeking to reconstitute the band with a permanent new frontman, but Vicious—McLaren's first choice—had sickened of him. In return for agreeing to record "My Way", Vicious had demanded that McLaren sign a sheet of paper declaring that he was no longer Vicious's manager. In August, Vicious, back in London, delivered his final performances as a nominal Sex Pistol: recording and filming cover versions of two <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Cochran" title="Eddie Cochran">Eddie Cochran</a> songs. The bassist's return to New York in September put paid to McLaren's dreaming.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_the_break-up">After the break-up</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex_Pistols&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: After the break-up">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After leaving the Pistols, Johnny Rotten reverted to his birth name of Lydon, and formed <a href="/wiki/Public_Image_Ltd." class="mw-redirect" title="Public Image Ltd.">Public Image Ltd.</a> (PiL) with former Clash member <a href="/wiki/Keith_Levene" title="Keith Levene">Keith Levene</a> and school friend <a href="/wiki/Jah_Wobble" title="Jah Wobble">Jah Wobble</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> The band went on to score a UK Top Ten hit with their debut single, 1978's "<a href="/wiki/Public_Image_(song)" title="Public Image (song)">Public Image</a>". Lydon initiated legal proceedings against McLaren and the Sex Pistols' management company, Glitterbest, which McLaren controlled. Among the claims were non-payment of royalties, improper usage of the title "Johnny Rotten", unfair contractual obligations,<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> and damages for "all the criminal activities that took place".<sup id="cite_ref-LV_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LV-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> In 1979, PiL recorded the <a href="/wiki/Post-punk" title="Post-punk">post-punk</a> classic <i><a href="/wiki/Metal_Box" title="Metal Box">Metal Box</a></i>. Lydon performed with the band through 1992, as well as engaging in other projects such as <a href="/wiki/Time_Zone_(band)" title="Time Zone (band)">Time Zone</a> with <a href="/wiki/Afrika_Bambaataa" title="Afrika Bambaataa">Afrika Bambaataa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bill_Laswell" title="Bill Laswell">Bill Laswell</a>.
</p><p>Vicious, relocated in New York, began performing as a solo artist, with Nancy Spungen acting as his manager. He recorded a live album, backed by "The Idols" featuring <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Kane" title="Arthur Kane">Arthur Kane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Nolan" title="Jerry Nolan">Jerry Nolan</a> of the New York Dolls—<i><a href="/wiki/Sid_Sings" title="Sid Sings">Sid Sings</a></i> was released in 1979. On 12 October 1978, Spungen was found dead in the <a href="/wiki/Hotel_Chelsea" title="Hotel Chelsea">Hotel Chelsea</a> room she was sharing with Vicious, with a stab wound to her stomach<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> and dressed only in her underwear.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcSV_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcSV-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Police recovered drug paraphernalia from the scene and Vicious was arrested and charged with her murder. In an interview at the time, McLaren said, "I can't believe he was involved in such a thing. Sid was set to marry Nancy in New York. He was very close to her and had quite a passionate affair with her."<sup id="cite_ref-bbcSV_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcSV-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Actor and heroin dealer <a href="/wiki/Rockets_Redglare" title="Rockets Redglare">Rockets Redglare</a>, who delivered pills to the apartment, has been mentioned as a possible alternative to Vicious as Spungen's killer.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While free on <a href="/wiki/Bail" title="Bail">bail</a>, Vicious smashed a beer mug in the face of Todd Smith, <a href="/wiki/Patti_Smith" title="Patti Smith">Patti Smith</a>'s brother, and was arrested again on an assault charge. On 9 December 1978 he was sent to <a href="/wiki/Rikers_Island" title="Rikers Island">Rikers Island</a> jail, where he spent 55 days and underwent enforced cold-turkey detox. He was released on 1 February 1979; sometime after midnight, following a small party to celebrate his release, Vicious died of a heroin overdose.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> He was twenty-one. Reflecting on the event, Lydon said, "Poor Sid. The only way he could live up to what he wanted everyone to believe about him was to die. That was tragic, but more for Sid than anyone else. He really bought his public image."<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 7 February 1979, just five days after Vicious's death, hearings began in London on Lydon's lawsuit. Cook and Jones were allied with McLaren, but as evidence mounted that their manager had poured virtually all of the band's revenue into his beloved film project, they switched sides. On 14 February, the court put the film and its soundtrack into <a href="/wiki/Receivership" title="Receivership">receivership</a>—no longer under McLaren's control, they were now to be administered as exploitable assets for addressing the band members' financial claims. McLaren, with substantial personal debts and legal fees, took off for Paris to sign a record deal for an LP of standards, including "<a href="/wiki/Non,_je_ne_regrette_rien" title="Non, je ne regrette rien">Non, je ne regrette rien</a>". A month later, back in London, he disassociated himself from the film to which he had devoted so much time and money.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> McLaren went on to carry out a one-month consultancy for <a href="/wiki/Adam_and_the_Ants" title="Adam and the Ants">Adam and the Ants</a> and manage their offshoot  <a href="/wiki/Bow_Wow_Wow" title="Bow Wow Wow">Bow Wow Wow</a>. In the mid-1980s he released a number of successful and influential records as a solo artist.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Rock_%27n%27_Roll_Swindle_(album)" title="The Great Rock &#39;n&#39; Roll Swindle (album)">The Great Rock 'n' Roll Swindle</a></i>, the soundtrack album for the still-uncompleted film, was released by Virgin Records on 24 February 1979. It is mostly composed of tracks credited to the Sex Pistols: There are the new recordings with vocals by Jones, Vicious, Cook, and Ronnie Biggs, as well as <a href="/wiki/Edward_Tudor-Pole" title="Edward Tudor-Pole">Edward Tudor-Pole</a>, who recorded a scene for the Sex Pistols' film. McLaren himself takes the mic for a couple of numbers. Several tracks feature Rotten's vocals from early, unissued sessions, in some cases with re-recorded backing by Jones and Cook. There is one live cut, from the band's final concert in San Francisco. The album is completed by a couple of tracks in which other artists cover Sex Pistols classics.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Four Top Ten singles were culled from the <i>Swindle</i> recordings, one more than had appeared on <i>Never Mind the Bollocks</i>. The 1978 "No One Is Innocent"/"My Way" was followed in 1979 by Vicious's cover of "<a href="/wiki/Somethin%27_Else_(Eddie_Cochran_song)" class="mw-redirect" title="Somethin&#39; Else (Eddie Cochran song)">Something Else</a>" (number three, and the biggest-selling single ever under the Sex Pistols name); Jones singing an original, "Silly Thing" (number six); and Vicious's second Cochran cover, "<a href="/wiki/C%27mon_Everybody" title="C&#39;mon Everybody">C'mon Everybody</a>" (number three). Two more singles from the soundtrack were put out under the Pistols brand—Tudor-Pole, among others, singing "The Great Rock 'n' Roll Swindle" and a Rotten vocal from 1976, "(I'm Not Your) Steppin' Stone"; both fell just shy of the Top Twenty.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> On 21 November 1980, the final "new" studio recordings attributed to the Sex Pistols were released by Virgin: "Black Leather" and "Here We Go Again", recorded by Jones and Cook during the mid-1978 <i>Swindle</i> sessions, were paired as one of a half-dozen 7-inch records (the other five reconfiguring previously released material) sold together as <i>Sex Pack</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sex Pistols film was completed by Temple, who received sole credit for the script after McLaren had his name taken off the production. Finally released in 1980, <i>The Great Rock 'n' Roll Swindle</i> still largely reflects McLaren's vision. It is a fictionalized, farcical, partially animated retelling of the band's history and aftermath with McLaren in the lead role, Jones as second lead, and contributions from Vicious (including his memorable performance of "My Way") and Cook. It incorporates promotional videos shot for "God Save the Queen" and "Pretty Vacant" and extensive documentary footage as well, much of it focusing  on Rotten. In Temple's description, he and McLaren conceived it as a "very stylized...polemic". They were reacting to the fact that the Pistols had become the "poster on the bedroom wall of the day where you kneel down last thing at night and pray to your rock god. And that was never the point.... The myth had to be dynamited in some way. We had to make this film in a way to enrage the fans".<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> In the film, McLaren claims to have created the band from scratch and engineered its notorious reputation; much of what structure the loose narrative has is based on McLaren's teaching a series of "lessons" to be learned from "an invention of mine they called the punk rock".<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Cook and Jones continued to work through guest appearances and as <a href="/wiki/Session_musician" title="Session musician">session musicians</a>. In 1980, they formed <a href="/wiki/The_Professionals_(band)" title="The Professionals (band)">The Professionals</a>, which lasted for two years. Jones went on to play with the bands Chequered Past and <a href="/wiki/Neurotic_Outsiders" title="Neurotic Outsiders">Neurotic Outsiders</a>. He also recorded two solo albums, <i><a href="/wiki/Mercy_(Steve_Jones_album)" title="Mercy (Steve Jones album)">Mercy</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Fire_and_Gasoline" title="Fire and Gasoline">Fire and Gasoline</a></i>. Now a resident of Los Angeles, he hosts a daily radio program called <i>Jonesy's Jukebox.</i> Having played with the band <a href="/wiki/Chiefs_of_Relief" title="Chiefs of Relief">Chiefs of Relief</a> in the late 1980s and with <a href="/wiki/Edwyn_Collins" title="Edwyn Collins">Edwyn Collins</a> in the 1990s,<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Cook is now a member of <a href="/wiki/Man_Raze" title="Man Raze">Man Raze</a>. Following The Rich Kids' break-up in 1979, Matlock played with various bands, toured with <a href="/wiki/Iggy_Pop" title="Iggy Pop">Iggy Pop</a>, and recorded several solo albums. He is currently a member of Slinky Vagabond.
</p><p>The 1979 court ruling had left many issues between Lydon and McLaren unresolved. Five years later, Lydon filed another action. Finally, on 16 January 1986, Lydon, Jones, Cook and the estate of Sid Vicious were awarded control of the band's heritage, including the rights to <i>The Great Rock 'n' Roll Swindle</i> and all the footage shot for it—more than 250 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> That same year, a fictionalised film account of Vicious's relationship with Spungen was released: <i><a href="/wiki/Sid_and_Nancy" title="Sid and Nancy">Sid and Nancy</a></i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Alex_Cox" title="Alex Cox">Alex Cox</a>. In his autobiography, Lydon lambastes the film, saying that it "celebrates heroin addiction", goes out of its way to "humiliate [Vicious's] life", and completely misrepresents the Sex Pistols' part in the London punk scene.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reunions_and_later_group_activities">Reunions and later group activities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex_Pistols&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Reunions and later group activities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hard_Rock_Cafe_London_11.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Hard_Rock_Cafe_London_11.JPG/220px-Hard_Rock_Cafe_London_11.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Hard_Rock_Cafe_London_11.JPG/330px-Hard_Rock_Cafe_London_11.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Hard_Rock_Cafe_London_11.JPG/440px-Hard_Rock_Cafe_London_11.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="4000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hard_Rock_Cafe_London_11.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fender Stratocaster signed by the Sex Pistols during their 1996 reunion tour, <a href="/wiki/Hard_Rock_Cafe" title="Hard Rock Cafe">Hard Rock Cafe</a>, London</div></div></div>
<p>The original four Sex Pistols reunited in 1996 for the six-month <a href="/wiki/Filthy_Lucre_Tour" title="Filthy Lucre Tour">Filthy Lucre Tour</a>, which included dates in Europe, North and South America, Australia and Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-RSE_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSE-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> The band members' access to the archives associated with <i>The Great Rock 'n' Roll Swindle</i> facilitated the production of the 2000 documentary <i><a href="/wiki/The_Filth_and_the_Fury" title="The Filth and the Fury">The Filth and the Fury</a></i>. This film—directed, like its predecessor, by Temple—was formulated as an attempt to tell the story from the band's point of view, in contrast to <i>Swindle'</i>s focus on McLaren and the media.<sup id="cite_ref-BW_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BW-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In 2002—the year of the Queen's <a href="/wiki/Golden_Jubilee" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Jubilee">Golden Jubilee</a>—the Sex Pistols reunited again to play the <a href="/wiki/Crystal_Palace_National_Sports_Centre" title="Crystal Palace National Sports Centre">Crystal Palace National Sports Centre</a> in London. In 2003, their Piss Off Tour took them around North America for three weeks.
</p><p>On 9 March 2006, the band sold the rights to their back catalogue to <a href="/wiki/Universal_Music_Group" title="Universal Music Group">Universal Music Group</a>. An anonymous  commentator for Australian newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/The_Age" title="The Age">The Age</a></i> called this a "sell out".<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> In November 2006, the Sex Pistols were inducted to the Rock and Roll Hall of Fame.<sup id="cite_ref-RRHF2_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RRHF2-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> The band rejected the honour in coarse language on their website. In a television interview, Lydon said the Hall of Fame could "<i>Kiss this!</i>" and made a rude gesture.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> According to Jones, "Once you want to be put into a museum, Rock &amp; Roll's over; it's not voted by fans, it's voted by people who induct you, or others; people who are already in it."<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sex Pistols reunited for five performances in the UK in November 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-SPR_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPR-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> In 2008, they undertook a series of European festival appearances, titled the Combine Harvester Tour. In August, after performing at the Dutch festival <a href="/wiki/A_Campingflight_to_Lowlands_Paradise" class="mw-redirect" title="A Campingflight to Lowlands Paradise">A Campingflight to Lowlands Paradise</a>, Lowlands director Eric van Eerdenburg declared the Pistols' performance "saddening": "They left their swimming pools at home only to scoop up some money here. Really, they're nothing more than that."<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> That same year, they released the DVD <i><a href="/wiki/There%27ll_Always_Be_An_England_(DVD)" class="mw-redirect" title="There&#39;ll Always Be An England (DVD)">There'll Always Be An England</a></i>, recorded at their Brixton Academy appearance on 10 November 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> In 2010, Fragrance and Beauty Limited announced the release of an authorised Sex Pistols scent. According to a statement from the cosmetics firm, "the fragrance exudes pure energy, pared down and pumped up by leather, shot through with heliotrope and brought back down to earth by a raunchy patchouli."<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> The band signed with <a href="/wiki/Universal_Music_Group" title="Universal Music Group">Universal Music Group</a> in 2012 to re-release <i>Never Mind the Bollocks</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 30 October 2018, former Sex Pistols members Steve Jones and Paul Cook joined up with <a href="/wiki/Billy_Idol" title="Billy Idol">Billy Idol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tony_James_(musician)" title="Tony James (musician)">Tony James</a>, both formerly of another first wave English punk rock band <a href="/wiki/Generation_X_(band)" title="Generation X (band)">Generation X</a>, to perform a free entry gig at <a href="/wiki/Roxy_Theatre_(West_Hollywood)" title="Roxy Theatre (West Hollywood)">The Roxy</a> in Hollywood, Los Angeles under the name Generation Sex, playing a combined set of the two former bands' material.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex_Pistols&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_influence">Cultural influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex_Pistols&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cultural influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Trouser_Press" title="Trouser Press">Trouser Press Record Guide</a></i> entry on the Sex Pistols remarks that "their importance—both to the direction of contemporary music and more generally to pop culture—can hardly be overstated".<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> <i>Rolling Stone</i> has argued that the band, "in direct opposition to the star trappings and complacency" of mid-1970s rock, came to spark and personify one of the few truly critical moments in pop culture—the rise of punk."<sup id="cite_ref-RSE_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSE-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> In 2004, the magazine ranked the Sex Pistols No.&#160;58 on its list of the "100 Greatest Artists of All Time."<sup id="cite_ref-BJA_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BJA-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Leading music critic <a href="/wiki/Dave_Marsh" title="Dave Marsh">Dave Marsh</a> called them "unquestionably the most radical new rock band of the Seventies."<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the Sex Pistols were not the first punk band, the few recordings that were released during the band's brief initial existence were singularly catalytic expressions of the punk movement. The releases of "Anarchy in the U.K.", "God Save the Queen" and <i>Never Mind the Bollocks</i> are counted among the most important events in the history of popular music. <i>Never Mind the Bollocks</i> is regularly cited in accountings of all-time great albums: In 2006, it was voted No.&#160;28 in <i><a href="/wiki/Q_(magazine)" title="Q (magazine)">Q</a></i> magazine's "100 Greatest Albums Ever",<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> while <i>Rolling Stone</i> listed it at No.&#160;2 in its 1987 "Top 100 Albums of the Last 20 Years".<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> It has come to be recognised as among the most influential records in rock history.<sup id="cite_ref-SPR_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPR-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/AllMusic" title="AllMusic">AllMusic</a> critic calls it "one of the greatest, most inspiring rock records of all time".<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sex Pistols directly inspired the style, and often the formation itself, of many punk and post-punk bands during their first two-and-a-half-year run. <a href="/wiki/The_Clash" title="The Clash">The Clash</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Siouxsie_and_the_Banshees" title="Siouxsie and the Banshees">Siouxsie and the Banshees</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Adverts" title="The Adverts">the Adverts</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vic_Godard" title="Vic Godard">Vic Godard</a> of <a href="/wiki/Subway_Sect" title="Subway Sect">Subway Sect</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ari_Up" title="Ari Up">Ari Up</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Slits" title="The Slits">the Slits</a><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> are among those in London's "inner circle" of early punk bands that credit the Pistols. <a href="/wiki/Pauline_Murray" title="Pauline Murray">Pauline Murray</a> of <a href="/wiki/County_Durham" title="County Durham">Durham</a> punk band <a href="/wiki/Penetration_(band)" title="Penetration (band)">Penetration</a> saw the Pistols perform for the first time in <a href="/wiki/Northallerton" title="Northallerton">Northallerton</a> in May 1976. She later explained their importance:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Nothing would have happened without the Pistols. It was like, "Wow, I believe in this." What they were saying was: "It's a load of shite. I'm going to do what I do and I don't care what people think." That was the key to it. People forget that, but it was the main ideology for me: we don't care what you think—you're shit anyway. It was the attitude that got people moving, as well as the music.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The Sex Pistols' 4 June 1976 concert at Manchester's Lesser Free Trade Hall was to become one of the most significant and mythologised events in rock history. Among the audience of merely forty people or so were many who became leading figures in the punk and post-punk movements: <a href="/wiki/Pete_Shelley" title="Pete Shelley">Pete Shelley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Howard_Devoto" title="Howard Devoto">Howard Devoto</a>, who organised the gig and were in the process of auditioning new members for the <a href="/wiki/Buzzcocks" title="Buzzcocks">Buzzcocks</a>; <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Sumner" title="Bernard Sumner">Bernard Sumner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ian_Curtis" title="Ian Curtis">Ian Curtis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Hook" title="Peter Hook">Peter Hook</a>, later of <a href="/wiki/Joy_Division" title="Joy Division">Joy Division</a>; <a href="/wiki/Mark_E._Smith" title="Mark E. Smith">Mark E. Smith</a>, later of <a href="/wiki/The_Fall_(band)" title="The Fall (band)">The Fall</a>; punk poet <a href="/wiki/John_Cooper_Clarke" title="John Cooper Clarke">John Cooper Clarke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morrissey" title="Morrissey">Morrissey</a>, later of <a href="/wiki/The_Smiths" title="The Smiths">The Smiths</a>. <a href="/wiki/Anthony_H._Wilson" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthony H. Wilson">Anthony H. Wilson</a>, founder of <a href="/wiki/Factory_Records" title="Factory Records">Factory Records</a>, saw the band for the first time at the return engagement on 20 July.<sup id="cite_ref-SPG_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPG-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Among the many musicians of a later time who have acknowledged their debt to the Pistols are members of the <a href="/wiki/The_Jesus_and_Mary_Chain" title="The Jesus and Mary Chain">Jesus and Mary Chain</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/NOFX" title="NOFX">NOFX</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Stone_Roses" title="The Stone Roses">The Stone Roses</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guns_N%27_Roses" title="Guns N&#39; Roses">Guns N' Roses</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(band)" title="Nirvana (band)">Nirvana</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Green_Day" title="Green Day">Green Day</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-BJA_200-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BJA-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Oasis_(band)" title="Oasis (band)">Oasis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> Calling the band "immensely influential", a <a href="/wiki/London_College_of_Music" title="London College of Music">London College of Music</a> study guide notes that "many styles of popular music, such as grunge, indie, thrash metal and even rap owe their foundations to the legacy of ground breaking punk bands—of which the Sex Pistols was the most prominent."<sup id="cite_ref-S&amp;S_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S&amp;S-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Ira Robbins of the <i>Trouser Press Record Guide</i>, "the Pistols and manager/provocateur Malcolm McLaren challenged every aspect and precept of modern music-making, thereby inspiring countless groups to follow their cue onto stages around the world. A confrontational, nihilistic public image and rabidly nihilistic socio-political lyrics set the tone that continues to guide punk bands."<sup id="cite_ref-IR_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IR-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Critic Toby Creswell locates the primary source of inspiration somewhat differently. Noting that "[i]mage to the contrary, the Pistols were very serious about music", he argues, "the real rebel yell came from Jones' guitars: a mass wall of sound based on the most simple, retro guitar riffs. Essentially, the Sex Pistols reinforced what the garage bands of the '60s had demonstrated—you don't need technique to make rock &amp; roll. In a time when music had been increasingly complicated and defanged, the Sex Pistols' generational shift caused a real revolution."<sup id="cite_ref-TC_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TC-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although much of the Sex Pistols' energy was directed against the establishment, not all of rock's elder statesmen dismissed them. <a href="/wiki/Pete_Townshend" title="Pete Townshend">Pete Townshend</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">the Who</a> said:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>When you listen to the Sex Pistols, to "Anarchy in the U.K." and "Bodies" and tracks like that, what immediately strikes you is that <i>this is actually happening." This is a bloke, with a brain on his shoulders, who is actually saying something he </i>sincerely<i> believes is happening in the world, saying it with real venom, and real passion. It touches you and it scares you—it makes you feel uncomfortable. It's like somebody saying, "The Germans are coming!  And there's no way we're gonna stop 'em!"<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup></i>
</p>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sid_vicious_madrid.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Sid_vicious_madrid.jpg/260px-Sid_vicious_madrid.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Sid_vicious_madrid.jpg/390px-Sid_vicious_madrid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Sid_vicious_madrid.jpg/520px-Sid_vicious_madrid.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sid_vicious_madrid.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An image of Vicious <i><a href="/wiki/Crying" title="Crying">lacrimosa</a></i> in Madrid, 2006</div></div></div>
<p>Along with their abundant musical influence, the Sex Pistols' cultural reverberations are evident elsewhere. Jamie Reid's work for the band is regarded as among the most important graphic design of the 1970s and still influences the field in the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> By the age of twenty-one, Sid Vicious was already a "t-shirt-selling icon".<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> While the manner of his death signified for many the inevitable failure of punk's social ambitions, it cemented his image as an archetype of doomed youth.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> British <a href="/wiki/Punk_fashion" title="Punk fashion">punk fashion</a>, still widely influential, is now customarily credited to Westwood and McLaren; as Johnny Rotten, Lydon had a lasting effect as well, especially through his <a href="/wiki/Bricolage" title="Bricolage">bricolage</a> approach to personal style: he "would wear a velvet collared drape jacket (<a href="/wiki/Teddy_boy" class="mw-redirect" title="Teddy boy">ted</a>) festooned with safety pins (<a href="/wiki/Jackie_Curtis" title="Jackie Curtis">Jackie Curtis</a> through the New York punk scene), massive pin-stripe pegs (modernist), a pin-collar Wemblex (<a href="/wiki/Mod_(subculture)#Fashion" title="Mod (subculture)">mod</a>) customised into an Anarchy shirt (punk) and <a href="/wiki/Brothel_creepers" class="mw-redirect" title="Brothel creepers">brothel creepers</a> (ted)."<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Nolan" title="Christopher Nolan">Christopher Nolan</a>, director of the <a href="/wiki/Batman" title="Batman">Batman</a> movie <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Knight_(film)" title="The Dark Knight (film)">The Dark Knight</a></i>, has said that Rotten inspired the characterization of <a href="/wiki/Joker_(comics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Joker (comics)">The Joker</a>, played by <a href="/wiki/Heath_Ledger" title="Heath Ledger">Heath Ledger</a>. According to Nolan, "We very much took the view in looking at the character of the Joker that what's strong about him is this idea of anarchy. This commitment to anarchy, this commitment to chaos."<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conceptual_basis_and_the_question_of_credit">Conceptual basis and the question of credit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex_Pistols&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Conceptual basis and the question of credit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Sex Pistols were defined by ambitions that went well beyond the musical—indeed, McLaren was at times openly contemptuous of the band's music and punk rock generally. "Christ, if people bought the records for the music, this thing would have died a death long ago," he said in 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> The degree to which the Pistols' anti-establishment stance resulted from the members' spontaneous attitudes as opposed to being cultivated by McLaren and his associates is a matter of debate—as is the very nature of that stance itself. Deprecating the music, McLaren elevated the concept, for which he later took full credit.
</p><p>He claimed that the Sex Pistols were his personal, Situationist-style art project: "I decided to use people, just the way a sculptor uses clay."<sup id="cite_ref-SR89_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SR89-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> But what had he supposedly made? The Sex Pistols were as substantial as pop culture could get: "Punk became the most important cultural phenomenon of the late 20th century", McLaren later asserted. "Its authenticity stands out against the karaoke ersatz culture of today, where everything and everyone is for sale.... [P]unk is not, and never was, for sale."<sup id="cite_ref-MM07_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MM07-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Or they were a cynical con: something with which "to sell trousers", as McLaren said in 1989;<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> a "carefully planned exercise to embezzle as much money as possible out of the music industry", as Jon Savage characterises McLaren's core theme in <i>The Great Rock 'n' Roll Swindle</i>;<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> "cash from chaos" as the movie repeatedly puts it.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lydon, in turn, dismissed McLaren's influence: "We made our own scandal just by being ourselves. Maybe it was that he knew he was redundant, so he overcompensated. All the talk about the French Situationists being associated with punk is bollocks. It's nonsense!"<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> Cook concurs: "Situationism had nothing to do with us. The Jamie Reids and Malcolms were excited because we were the real thing. I suppose we were what they were dreaming of."<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> According to Lydon, "If we had an aim, it was to force our own, working-class opinions into the mainstream, which was unheard of in pop music at the time."<sup id="cite_ref-LV_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LV-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Toby Creswell argues that the "Sex Pistols' agenda was inchoate, to say the least. It was a general call to rebellion that falls apart at the slightest scrutiny."<sup id="cite_ref-TC_219-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TC-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Critic Ian Birch, writing in 1981, called "stupid" the claim that the Sex Pistols "had any political significance.... If they did anything, they made a lot of people content with being nothing. They certainly didn't inspire the working classes."<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> While the <a href="/wiki/1979_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1979 United Kingdom general election">Conservative triumph in 1979</a> may be taken as evidence for that position, Julien Temple has noted that the scene inspired by the Sex Pistols "wasn't your kind of two-up, two-down working class normal families, most of it. It was over the edge of the precipice in social terms. They were actually giving a voice to an area of the working class that was almost beyond the pale."<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> Within a year of "Anarchy in the U.K." that voice was being echoed widely: scores if not hundreds of punk bands had formed across the country—groups composed largely of working-class members or middle-class members who rejected their own class values and pursued solidarity with the working class.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1980, critic <a href="/wiki/Greil_Marcus" title="Greil Marcus">Greil Marcus</a> reflected on McLaren's contradictory posture:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>It may be that in the mind of their self-celebrated Svengali...the Sex Pistols were never meant to be more than a nine-month wonder, a cheap vehicle for some fast money, a few laughs, a touch of the old <i><a href="/wiki/%C3%89pater_la_bourgeoisie" title="Épater la bourgeoisie">épater la bourgeoisie</a></i>. It may also be that in the mind of their chief terrorist and propagandist, anarchist veteran...and Situational artist McLaren, the Sex Pistols were meant to be a force that would set the world on its ear...and finally unite music and politics. The Sex Pistols were all of these things.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>A couple of years before, Marcus had identified different roots underlying the band's merger of music and politics, arguing that they "have absorbed from <a href="/wiki/Reggae" title="Reggae">reggae</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rastafari_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Rastafari movement">Rastas</a> the idea of a culture that will make demands on those in power which no government could ever satisfy; a culture that will be exclusive, almost separatist, yet also messianic, apocalyptic and stoic, and that will ignore or smash any contradiction inherent in such a complexity of stances."<sup id="cite_ref-PN_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PN-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Critic Sean Campbell has discussed how Lydon's Irish Catholic heritage both facilitated his entrée into London's reggae scene and complicated his position for the ethnically English working class—the background his bandmates had in common.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sex_Pistols_in_Paradiso_-_Johnny_Rotten_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Sex_Pistols_in_Paradiso_-_Johnny_Rotten_2.jpg/220px-Sex_Pistols_in_Paradiso_-_Johnny_Rotten_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Sex_Pistols_in_Paradiso_-_Johnny_Rotten_2.jpg/330px-Sex_Pistols_in_Paradiso_-_Johnny_Rotten_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Sex_Pistols_in_Paradiso_-_Johnny_Rotten_2.jpg/440px-Sex_Pistols_in_Paradiso_-_Johnny_Rotten_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="3676" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sex_Pistols_in_Paradiso_-_Johnny_Rotten_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Johnny Rotten on stage in Paradiso, Amsterdam, January 1977</div></div></div>
<p>Critic Bill Wyman acknowledges that Lydon's "fierce intelligence and astonishing onstage charisma" were important catalysts, but ultimately finds the band's real meaning lies in McLaren's provocative media manipulations.<sup id="cite_ref-BW_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BW-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> While some of the Sex Pistols' public affronts were plotted by McLaren, Westwood, and company, others were evidently not—including what McLaren himself cites as the "pivotal moment that changed everything",<sup id="cite_ref-MM07_227-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MM07-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> the clash on the Bill Grundy <i>Today</i> show.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> "Malcolm milked situations", says Cook, "he didn't instigate them; that was always our own doing."<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> It is also hard to ascribe the effect of the Sex Pistols' early Manchester shows on that city's nascent punk scene to anyone other than the musicians themselves. Matlock later wrote that at the point when he left the band, it was beginning to occur to him that McLaren "was in fact quite deliberately perpetrating that idea of us as his puppets.... However, on the other hand, I've since found out that even Malcolm wasn't as aware of what he was up to as he has since made out."<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> By his absence, Matlock demonstrated how crucial he was to the band's creativity: in the eleven months between his departure and the Pistols' demise, they composed only two songs.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Music historian <a href="/wiki/Simon_Reynolds" title="Simon Reynolds">Simon Reynolds</a> argues that McLaren came into his own as an <a href="/wiki/Auteur" title="Auteur">auteur</a> only after the group's break-up, with <i>The Great Rock 'n' Roll Swindle</i> and the recruitment of Ronnie Biggs as a vocalist.<sup id="cite_ref-SR89_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SR89-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Much subsequent commentary on the Sex Pistols has relied on taking seriously McLaren's onscreen proclamations in the film, whether lending them credence or not. As music journalist Dave Thompson noted in 2000, "[T]oday, <i>Swindle</i> is viewed by many as the truth"<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> (despite the fact that the movie purveys, among other things, a completely illiterate Steve Jones, a talking dog, and Sid Vicious shooting audience members, including his mother, at the conclusion of "My Way"). Temple points out that McLaren's characterization was intended as "a big fucking joke—that he was the puppetmeister who created these pieces of clay from plasticine boxes that he modeled away and made Johnny Rotten, made Sid Vicious. It was a <i>joke</i> that they were completely manufactured."<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> (In his final onscreen scene in the film, McLaren declares that he was planning the Sex Pistols affair, "Ever since I was ten years old! Ever since <a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a> joined the army!" [1956 and 1958, respectively].)<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Temple acknowledges that McLaren ultimately "perhaps took this too much to heart."<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Pistols tour manager Noel Monk and journalist Jimmy Guterman, Lydon was much more than "the band's mouthpiece. He's its raging brain. McLaren or his friend Jamie Reid might drop a word like 'anarchy' or 'vacant' that Rotten seizes upon and turns into a manifesto, but McLaren is not the Svengali to Rotten he'd like to be perceived as. McLaren thought he was working with a tabula rasa, but he soon found out that Rotten has ideas of his own".<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, there is little disagreement about McLaren's marketing talent and his crucial role in making the band a <a href="/wiki/Subculture" title="Subculture">subcultural</a> phenomenon soon after its debut.<sup id="cite_ref-BW_187-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BW-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> Temple adds that "he catalyzed so many people's heads. He had so many just extraordinary ideas".<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> Though, as Jon Savage emphasises, "In fact, it was Steve Jones who first had the idea of putting the group, or any group, together with McLaren. He chose McLaren, not vice versa."<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Band_members">Band members</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex_Pistols&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Band members">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div>

&#32;
<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:100%;"><tbody><tr>
<td style="width: 50%;text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">
<dl><dt>Official members</dt></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/John_Lydon" title="John Lydon">Johnny Rotten</a>&#160;– lead vocals <small>(1975–1978, 1996–2001, 2002–2003, 2007–2008)</small></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Steve_Jones_(musician)" title="Steve Jones (musician)">Steve Jones</a>&#160;– guitar, backing vocals <small>(1975–1978, 1996–2001, 2002–2003, 2007–2008)</small></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Glen_Matlock" title="Glen Matlock">Glen Matlock</a>&#160;– bass, backing vocals <small>(1975–1977, 1996–2001, 2002–2003, 2007–2008)</small></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paul_Cook" title="Paul Cook">Paul Cook</a>&#160;– drums <small>(1975–1978, 1996–2001, 2002–2003, 2007–2008)</small></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sid_Vicious" title="Sid Vicious">Sid Vicious</a>&#160;– bass, backing vocals <small>(1977–1978; died 1979)</small></li></ul>
<p>&#32;
</p>
</td>
<td style="width: 50%;text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">
<dl><dt>Guest musicians</dt></dl>
<p>Musicians other than the band members who recorded songs with Steve Jones and Paul Cook on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Rock_%27n%27_Roll_Swindle_(album)" title="The Great Rock &#39;n&#39; Roll Swindle (album)">The Great Rock 'n' Roll Swindle</a></i>:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ronnie_Biggs" title="Ronnie Biggs">Ronnie Biggs</a>&#160;– lead vocals on "<a href="/wiki/No_One_Is_Innocent" title="No One Is Innocent">No One Is Innocent</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Belsen_Was_a_Gas" title="Belsen Was a Gas">Belsen Was a Gas</a>"</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Edward_Tudor-Pole" title="Edward Tudor-Pole">Edward Tudor-Pole</a>&#160;– lead vocals on "The Great Rock 'n' Roll Swindle", "Who Killed Bambi?", "<a href="/wiki/Rock_Around_the_Clock" title="Rock Around the Clock">Rock Around the Clock</a>"</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Malcolm_McLaren" title="Malcolm McLaren">Malcolm McLaren</a>&#160;– lead vocals on "God Save The Queen (Symphony)", "You Need Hands"</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dave_Goodman_(record_producer)" title="Dave Goodman (record producer)">Dave Goodman</a>&#160;– bass on "The Great Rock 'n' Roll Swindle"</li>
<li>Andy Allan&#160;– bass on "Silly Thing (single version)"</li></ul>
<p>&#32;
</p>

<li id="cite_note-165"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-165">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Gimarc, George, <i>Punk Diary</i>, p. 145. Gimarc refers to sources claiming that the "My Way" recording involved no contact between Vicious and the Jones-Cook duo; Temple, however, says that Jones was flown over to Paris to join Vicious in the studio (Temple, Julien, "Commentary", 1:29:18–1:29:20), and seems to indicate that he recorded his guitar part there (1:33:09–1:33:16).</span>

Savage describes the single as being a double A-side; other sources indicate that the Biggs vocal was the A-side and the Vicious vocal the B-side (e.g., Gimarc, George, <i>Punk Diary</i>, p. 145). There is no disagreement that the Vicious side was the more popular.</span>

<li id="cite_note-238"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-238">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See, for instance, Temple's commentary: "[It] was not planned at all. It was totally spontaneous. And as the band will tell you, Malcolm said, 'You've blown it. Concerning the time the band spent waiting to go on air, Siouxsie Sioux later said, "I've got a feeling that Malcolm <i>was</i> geeing them up, stirring it a bit" (Savage, Jon, <i>England's Dreaming</i>, p. 257). Her view is belied by the version of the incident in Phil Strongman's <i>Pretty Vacant</i>, which appears to rely on McLaren himself (pp. 154–155). <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sex_Pistols&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Julie_Burchill" title="Julie Burchill">Burchill, Julie</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tony_Parsons_(British_journalist)" title="Tony Parsons (British journalist)">Tony Parsons</a>, <i>The Boy Looked at Johnny: The Obituary of Rock and Roll</i>, Pluto Press, 1978. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-571-12992-7" title="Special:BookSources/0-571-12992-7">0-571-12992-7</a></li>
<li>Colegrave, Stephen, and Chris Sullivan, <i>Punk: The Definitive Record of a Revolution</i>, Thunder's Mouth Press, 2005. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1-56025-769-5" title="Special:BookSources/1-56025-769-5">1-56025-769-5</a></li>
<li>Coon, Caroline, <i>1988: The New Wave Punk Rock Explosion</i>, Omnibus Press, 1977. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-7119-0051-5" title="Special:BookSources/0-7119-0051-5">0-7119-0051-5</a></li>
<li>Dalton, David, <i>El Sid Saint Vicious</i>, St. Martin's Press, 1997. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-312-15520-4" title="Special:BookSources/0-312-15520-4">0-312-15520-4</a></li>
<li>Morris, Dennis, <i>Destroy: Sex Pistols 1977</i>, Creation Books, 2002. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1-84068-058-X" title="Special:BookSources/1-84068-058-X">1-84068-058-X</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/David_Nolan_(British_author)" title="David Nolan (British author)">Nolan, David</a>, <i>I Swear I Was There: The Gig That Changed the World</i>, IMP Books, 2006 [2001]. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-9549704-9-7" title="Special:BookSources/0-9549704-9-7">0-9549704-9-7</a></li>
<li>Parker, Alan, "No One Is Innocent: Sid Vicious" 2001 [Orion Books]</li>
<li>Parker, Alan, <i>Young Flesh Required: Growing Up With the Sex Pistols</i>, Soundcheck Books, 2011. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-9566420-1-2" title="Special:BookSources/0-9566420-1-2">0-9566420-1-2</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gavin_Walsh" title="Gavin Walsh">Walsh, Gavin</a>, <i>God Save the Sex Pistols: A Collector's Guide to the Priests Of Punk</i>, Plexus, 2003. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-85965-316-1" title="Special:BookSources/0-85965-316-1">0-85965-316-1</a></li></ul>
